Hopkins v. Hopkins


No. M2002-02233-SC-R11-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


152 S.W.3d 447; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 830


June 2, 2004, Session, Heard at Cookeville 1 


1 Oral argument was heard in this case on June 2, 2004, in Cookeville, Putnam County, Tennessee, as part of this Court's S.C.A.L.E.S. (Supreme Court Advancing Legal Education for Students) project.

 
September 27, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected September 28, 2004.
Rehearing denied by, Motion denied by Synthia M. Hopkins v. Victor L. Hopkins, 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 1065 (Tenn., Dec. 2, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Appeal by Permission; Judgment of the Court of Appeals Affirmed in Part and Vacated in Part; Case Remanded to the Trial Court. Appeal by Permission from the Court of Appeals Chancery Court for Montgomery County. No. 2001-01-0072. Carol Catalano, Chancellor.
Hopkins v. Hopkins, 2003 Tenn. App. LEXIS 460 (Tenn. Ct. App., June 25, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in Part and Vacated in Part; Case Remanded to the Trial Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a divorce action between appellant father and appellee mother, the trial court rejected proposed parenting plans, effectively granted primary physical custody of the parties' children to the father, and ordered the mother to pay child support. The Tennessee Court of Appeals reversed, divided child custody between the parties equally, but ordered the father to pay child support. The instant court granted the father permission to appeal.


OVERVIEW: The father and the mother were in agreement as to equal parenting time. The Tennessee Supreme Court first rejected the father's assertion that the income of both parents should have been considered in calculating the child support award. Under Tenn. Comp. R. & Regs. 1240-2-4-.03(2), the income of the parent with whom the children primarily lived was not to be considered in the calculation of child support. However, the primary issue on appeal was whether child support could be awarded when neither party had been designated the primary residential parent. The supreme court noted that the Tennessee Child Support Guidelines contemplated that child support could be awarded only to the primary residential parent. Moreover, other state and federal statutes required that one parent be named a child's custodian. Thus, the court of appeals erred in failing to designate a primary residential parent and in awarding child support without designating same. Consequently, a remand was necessary. The trial court was directed to consider all of the relevant statutory factors in designating a primary residential parent, in calculating child support, and in entering a parenting plan.


OUTCOME: The matter was remanded for further proceedings in accordance with the supreme court's opinion. Costs on appeal were taxed equally to the parties.

 

125 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


City of Memphis v. Shelby County Election Comm'n


No. W2004-02182-SC-RDM-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT JACKSON


146 S.W.3d 531; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 802



September 15, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication September 15, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. Code Ann. § 16-3-201; Judgment of the Trial Court Reversed. Appeal from the Chancery Court of Shelby County. No. CH-04-1736-II. Arnold B. Goldin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Trial Court Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee county election commission refused to place a referendum ordinance on the election ballot based upon the state election coordinator's opinion that the ordinance was unconstitutional. Appellant city sought injunctive relief, challenging the commission's refusal. The Chancery Court of Shelby County, Tennessee, refused to grant injunctive relief. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: In refusing to grant injunctive relief, the trial court concluded that the commission had the authority to refuse to place the ordinance on the ballot; it also found that the ordinance would have been unconstitutional because it would have unlawfully increased the taxing power of the city. On review, the city argued that the commission and the coordinator were ministerial officials who had no authority to refuse to place the ordinance on the ballot because they believed it to be unconstitutional. The appellate court agreed, concluding that determining the substantive constitutionality of such measures was a function reserved for the judiciary. Regardless of whether the ordinance would have been unconstitutional, the coordinator and the commission violated the constitutional principles of separation of powers by refusing to place the ordinance on the ballot. Further, a challenge to the substantive validity of the ordinance was not ripe for judicial determination, as the ordinance was not yet approved by the voters.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the commission was ordered to include the ordinance on the ballot.

 

126 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


State v. Leach


No. M2001-01421-SC-DDT-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


148 S.W.3d 42; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 741


June 1, 2004, Argued  
September 8, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Leach, 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 955 (Tenn., Nov. 8, 2004)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Leach v. Tennessee, 544 U.S. 966, 125 S. Ct. 1739, 161 L. Ed. 2d 610, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 3045 (2005)
Post-conviction relief denied at Leach v. State, 2010 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 443 (Tenn. Crim. App., June 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Automatic Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Davidson County. No. 99-D-2508 J. Randall Wyatt, Judge.
State v. Leach, 2003 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 724 (Tenn. Crim. App., Aug. 25, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed, and remanded for correction of clerical errors.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree premeditated murder, two counts of first degree felony murder, one count of especially aggravated robbery, and one count of aggravated rape. The trial court merged the felony murder convictions with the premeditated murder convictions. The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the evidence was insufficient to support the convictions for premeditated murder because the State failed to prove premeditation. The supreme court disagreed. The evidence, when examined in the light most favorable to the State, was sufficient to support a finding of premeditation. Defendant inflicted multiple wounds upon each victim during the particularly cruel killings. He used two weapons - scissors and a ligature - on each of the unarmed victims and attempted to dispose of evidence by flushing his underwear down the toilet. Defendant exhibited calmness after the murders by showering, changing his clothes, looking for valuables, and then driving to Missouri where he socialized with friends at a tavern that night. Although defendant made no prior threats or declarations of intent to kill the victim's, he told a witness that he had always had a secret fantasy of committing multiple rapes and homicides. Even without that testimony, however, the jury could have reasonably inferred that defendant acted with premeditation when he killed the victims to obtain the means of facilitating his flight and to prevent them from alerting the police.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. The case was remanded for the correction of clerical errors.

State v. Davis


No.M2001-01866-SC-DDT-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


141 S.W.3d 600; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 663


June 3, 2004, Argued  
August 25, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication August 25, 2004.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Davis v. Tennessee, 543 U.S. 1156, 125 S. Ct. 1306, 161 L. Ed. 2d 123, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 1599 (2005)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Decision reached on appeal by Davis v. State, 2012 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 672 (Tenn. Crim. App., Aug. 24, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-206 Death Penalty Appeal; Judgment of the Criminal Court Affirmed. Automatic Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Davidson County. No. 96-B-866. J. Randall Wyatt, Judge.
State v. Davis, 2003 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 250 (Tenn. Crim. App., Mar. 25, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Davidson County (Tennessee), which affirmed his conviction of two counts of premeditated first-degree murder, two counts of especially aggravated kidnapping, and two counts of especially aggravated robbery, and sentenced him to death. After the appeals court affirmed the convictions and sentence, the case was automatically docketed in the court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was 18 years old, participated in a "fake gun deal," wherein he and accomplices stole guns from victims, tied them up in their car, and then drove them to a construction site where defendant shot them. Defendant was convicted of premeditated first-degree murder, aggravated kidnapping, and aggravated robbery. He was sentenced to death. The appeals court affirmed. Upon review, the court also affirmed, finding that the evidence was sufficient to support the jury's verdicts, as it showed that defendant had said that the victims would have to be killed because they knew who defendant and his accomplices were and where they lived, and, shortly before the offenses, defendant obtained a nine millimeter handgun that was used in the shootings. Also, defendant and his accomplice were seen driving the victims' car on the night of the offenses with the victims tied up in it and were in possession of guns and the victim's personal clothing and jewelry. The court also found that the evidence showed that defendant kidnapped the victims with the use or display of deadly weapons, and intentionally took their property by using a deadly weapon and by causing serious bodily injury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

State v. Berry


No. M2001-02023-SC-DDT-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


141 S.W.3d 549; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 659



August 23, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Released for Publication August 23, 2004.
Application denied by Berry v. State, 2007 Tenn. LEXIS 711 (Tenn., Aug. 13, 2007)
Post-conviction relief denied at, in part Berry v. State, 366 S.W.3d 160, 2011 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 812 (Tenn. Crim. App., 2011)
Writ denied by Berry v. State, 2016 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 214 (Tenn. Crim. App., Mar. 23, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: Automatic Appeal pursuant to Tennessee Code Annotated § 39-13-206(a)(1); Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed. Automatic Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Davidson County. No. 96-B-866. J. Randall Wyatt, Judge.
State v. Berry, 2003 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 316 (Tenn. Crim. App., Apr. 10, 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of premeditated murder, felony murder, especially aggravated kidnapping, and especially aggravated robbery, and was sentenced to death. After the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Davidson County (Tennessee) affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence, an automatic appeal was docketed pursuant to Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-206(a)(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for the murders, kidnappings, and robberies of the two victims. Defendant and his co-defendant killed the victims after robbing them of weapons they had arranged to purchase. On appeal, the court held that the indictment against defendant was not constitutionally defective due to the fact that it failed to charge the aggravating circumstances relied on by the State to sentence him to death, because the court had consistently rejected the argument that aggravating circumstances had to be pled in the indictment. The court also held that Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-204(c), the evidentiary standard used during sentencing, did not violate the due process or confrontation clauses of the Fifth and Sixth Amendments. The court held that the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's conviction of first-degree premeditated murder, as the evidence showed that defendant and his co-defendant decided to rob the victims instead of paying for the weapons, defendant was overheard saying that, if they robbed the victims, they would have to kill them, because they knew defendants, and they carried handcuffs, rope, and duct tape to the meeting.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and death sentence were affirmed.

State v. Odom


No. W2000-02301-SC-DDT-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT JACKSON


137 S.W.3d 572; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 452


November 12, 2003, Session, Heard at Memphis  
May 21, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 20, 2004
Later proceeding at, Remanded by State v. Odom, 2007 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 305 (Tenn. Crim. App., Apr. 13, 2007)
Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by State v. Odom, 2010 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 223 (Tenn. Crim. App., Mar. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-206(a)(1); Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Reversed. Automatic Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Shelby County. No. 91-07049. Chris Craft, Judge.
State v. Odom, 2002 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 871 (Tenn. Crim. App., Oct. 15, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of felony murder and sentenced to death. After a remand for resentencing, he was again sentenced to death. The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by applying a 1998 amendment to Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-204(c), and thereby allowing the State to introduce specifics of defendant's prior violent crimes at sentencing. The supreme court agreed. Section 39-13-204(c) (1998) was not to be applied to cases occurring before its effective date. The trial court's erroneous application of the 1998 amendment led directly to the prosecution's introduction of detailed and graphic evidence of prior violent felonies committed by defendant. The law as it existed at the time of the offense prohibited such evidence. Moreover, the prosecution heavily relied upon the inadmissible evidence underlying defendant's prior felonies in arguing that the jury should impose the death penalty for defendant's offense. In short, under the circumstances, the error was not harmless because it affected the verdict.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for resentencing.

 

130 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


Thompson v. State


No. M1987-00067-SC-DPE-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


134 S.W.3d 168; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 370


April 8, 2004, Assigned on Briefs  
May 12, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 12, 2004.
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Thompson v. Bell, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 27283 (E.D. Tenn., May 4, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Direct Appeal from the Circuit Court for Coffee County. No. 20,014. Gerald L. Ewell, Sr., Judge.
State v. Thompson, 768 S.W.2d 239, 1989 Tenn. LEXIS 47 (Tenn., 1989)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Circuit Court Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision of the Circuit Court for Coffee County (Tennessee), which denied defendant a hearing on the issue of his competency to be executed. Also before the court were (1) a motion by defendant to vacate the trial court's order and remand for designation of another judge, (2) a motion to reconsider the court's previous denial of a motion to withdraw by defendant's co-counsel, and (3) a motion requesting a stay of execution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had been convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to death. He sought a hearing on the issue of his competency to be executed, but the trial court denied the request. The court affirmed. The court refused to reconsider its prior decision denying defendant's co-counsel's motion to withdraw because there was nothing requiring withdrawal. The court refused to remand the matter for the designation of another judge. A judge's recusal in defendant's post-conviction case did not disqualify him from presiding in this competency proceeding. The court refused to grant a stay. The trial court did not err in concluding that defendant failed to make a threshold showing sufficient to have warranted a hearing on his competency petition. The reports of defendant's mental health experts showed that, despite any delusions, defendant understood that he was going to be executed for murdering the victim. While defendant professed unorthodox views about what was going to happen after his execution, these beliefs did not vitiate defendant's awareness of the fact of his execution and the reason for it. Thus, no issues existed regarding his competency to be executed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision and denied all motions.

 

131 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


City of Cookeville v. Humphrey


No. M2001-00695-SC-R11-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


126 S.W.3d 897; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 130


October 1, 2003 Session  
February 20, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Appeal by Permission; Judgment of the Court of Appeals Affirmed. Appeal by Permission from the Court of Appeals Chancery Court for Putnam County No. 99-219 Billy Joe White, Chancellor.
City of Cookeville v. Humphrey, 2002 Tenn. App. LEXIS 817 (Tenn. Ct. App., Nov. 20, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hospital authority sought a declaration that it had the authority to enter into an exclusive contract for professional imaging services. Defendant radiologists, who currently had clinical privileges at the hospital's imaging department, filed a counterclaim. The trial court granted the hospital authority's motion for summary judgment and the Court of Appeals (Tennessee) affirmed the trial court's judgment. The radiologists appealed.


OVERVIEW: The radiologists applied for a certificate of need to perform outpatient diagnostic imaging services in competition with the hospital. The hospital authority's board of trustees decided to oppose the certificate of need application. The medical staff of the hospital voted to support the radiologists' company in becoming the providers of radiology services under an exclusive provider contract if an agreement could be reached. The company objected to several provisions in the request for a proposal. The hospital authority filed an action for declaratory judgment seeking a declaration of the right of the hospital to close the staff of its imaging department by means of an exclusive provider contract. The appellate court found that the Hospital Authority Act permitted the hospital authority to close the staff of the hospital's imaging department by means of an exclusive provider contract. Also, the board's decision to close the staff of the hospital's imaging department did not violate the medical staff bylaws. Finally, the radiologists were not legally or constitutionally entitled to a hearing if their clinical privileges were terminated upon the execution of such a contract.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.

 

132 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


Doe v. Doe


No. M2003-01142-SC-S25-BP


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


127 S.W.3d 728; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 128



February 19, 2004, Filed



DISPOSITION: Petition for contempt denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney filed a petition for contempt against respondent attorney, alleging violations of the confidentiality requirement of Tenn. Sup. Ct. R. 9, § 25.


OVERVIEW: Respondent sent a letter responding to allegations against her and accusing petitioner of unethical conduct. Respondent sent copies of the letter to two other attorneys, a judge and a trial court clerk. Petitioner alleged that respondent's conduct violated the confidentiality requirement of Tenn. Sup. Ct. R. 9, § 25. Respondent argued that § 25 violated her constitutional right of free speech. The supreme court agreed. The three interests addressed by § 25 - protection of the reputation of an attorney and the bar from meritless complaints, protection of anonymity of complainants and other persons supplying information, and maintenance of the integrity of pending investigations - while legitimate, were not sufficiently compelling to justify the restriction on free speech by § 25, particularly considering the broad scope of its confidentiality requirement. Consequently, § 25 violated free speech rights under Tenn. Const. art. I, § 19 and the First Amendment.


OUTCOME: The petition was denied.

 

133 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


Carpenter v. State


No. E2001-01732-SC-R11-PC


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT KNOXVILLE


126 S.W.3d 879; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 24


September 4, 2003, Session  
January 13, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 13, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Appeal by Permission; Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed. Appeal by Permission from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Knox County. No. 47388. Richard R. Baumgartner, Judge.
Carpenter v. State, 2002 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 995 (Tenn. Crim. App., Nov. 20, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree felony murder. The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Knox County affirmed. The court denied defendant's motion for postconviction relief. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by denying his motion for postconviction relief because appellate counsel were deficient for failing to challenge the trial court's refusal to instruct the jury on second-degree murder as a lesser-included offense. The supreme court disagreed. Some fairly strong issues were raised on appeal. Given the confusion regarding the proper standard to apply in determining when lesser-included offense instructions were required, it was unclear whether the omitted issue would have resulted in reversal on appeal if it had been raised. In light of the facts of the case and the precedent available at the time of the appeal, the supreme court could not conclude that the omitted issue was "clearly stronger" than those presented. Trial counsel objected to the trial court's failure to instruct on lesser-included offenses, although he was not required to do so. Appellate counsel testified at length about the strategy on appeal. In determining what issues to raise, counsel chose those issues that were most likely to achieve success. Appellate counsel were experienced attorneys and defendant did not ask them to raise any specific issues on appeal.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

134 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


State v. Holton


No. M2000-00766-SC-DDT-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


126 S.W.3d 845; 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 1


October 3, 2003, Session, Heard at Murfreesboro 1


1 This case was heard as part of the October 3, 2003, S.C.A.L.E.S. (Supreme Court Advancing Legal Education for Students) project in Murfreesboro, Rutherford County, Tennessee.

 
January 5, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing denied by State v. Holton, 2004 Tenn. LEXIS 129 (Tenn., Feb. 17, 2004)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Holton v. Tennessee, 543 U.S. 816, 125 S. Ct. 62, 160 L. Ed. 2d 22, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 5611 (2004)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Holton v. State, 2006 Tenn. LEXIS 331 (Tenn., May 4, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-206(a)(1); Judgments of the Trial Court and Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed. No. 14302. William Charles Lee, Judge. Direct Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals Circuit Court for Bedford County.
State v. Holton, 2002 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 572 (Tenn. Crim. App., July 17, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of four counts of first-degree premeditated murder. The jury imposed a sentence of death on each conviction. The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his convictions. The supreme court disagreed. Defendant admitted that he had contemplated and planned to murder his four young children. He told the police that he had been thinking about killing his children for two years. He explained that he definitively resolved to kill his children after speaking with his former wife two days before the killings occurred. Defendant formulated the plan, he concealed the loaded gun at the garage, he called his former wife to let her know that he would be taking the children by the garage on the pretense of showing them a dog, he lured the children, two at a time, to the rear bay of the garage promising them a surprise, he positioned the children so that one shot would pierce the hearts of both, he gave the two younger children a drill and a hammer to play with so they would be distracted and not hear their brothers being shot, and he concealed the bodies of his older sons before persuading the two younger children to come to the rear bay of the garage. Therefore, the evidence was sufficient to sustain defendant's convictions.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Rutherford County v. Wilson


No. M2000-01382-SC-R11-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


2003 Tenn. LEXIS 1261



December 29, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Rutherford County v. Wilson, 121 S.W.3d 591, 2003 Tenn. LEXIS 1023 (Tenn., 2003)

 

136 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


Wallace v. State


No. M2001-02722-SC-R11-PC


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


121 S.W.3d 652; 2003 Tenn. LEXIS 1213


October 1, 2003, Session  
December 23, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 23, 2003.
On remand at Wallace v. State, 2005 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 89 (Tenn. Crim. App., Jan. 31, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] An Appeal by Permission from the Court of Criminal Appeals Circuit Court for Stewart County. No. 4-769-CR-96. Robert E. Burch, Judge Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Appeal by Permission; Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Reversed; Case Remanded to the Court of Criminal Appeals.
Wallace v. State, 2002 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 1050 (Tenn. Crim. App., Dec. 9, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was granted a delayed direct appeal of a first degree murder conviction due to trial counsel's failure to file a motion for a new trial that resulted in a waiver of issues on direct appeal. The Court of Criminal Appeals Circuit Court for Stewart County (Tennessee) dismissed defendant's subsequent appeal, concluding that the trial court lacked the authority to grant that form of postconviction relief. Defendant appealed the judgment.


OVERVIEW: Contrary to the lower court's conclusion, defendant could have received a delayed appeal under Tenn. Code Ann. §§ 40-30-113(a)(1) & (3) and 40-30-111(a) if he had been denied ineffective assistance of counsel in violation of the Sixth Amendment or Tenn. Const. art. I, § 9. In this case, trial counsel's failure to file a timely motion for a new trial and withdraw to allow defendant to file a pro se motion was deficient performance as that failure resulted in the waiver of post-trial and appellate remedies and violated counsel's duties under Tenn. R. Crim. P. 37(e). The fact that defendant was entitled to direct review on the issue of the sufficiency of the evidence did not resolve the prejudice prong of the Strickland test as nothing in Tenn. Code Ann. § 40-30-113 limited the trial court's discretion to order a delayed direct appeal to circumstances where there was a complete denial of such an appeal. Defendant was not required to show actual prejudice because counsel's failure to preserve and pursue available post-trial remedies was presumptively prejudicial. Thus, counsel's deficiencies post-trial amounted to a complete failure to subject the State's case to appellate scrutiny.


OUTCOME: The lower court's judgment was reversed, the trial court's grant of a delayed appeal was affirmed, and the case was remanded to the lower court for review of the issues presented by defendant's appeal from the denial of his motion for a new trial. Costs were taxed to the State.

 

137 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


In re D.L.B.


No. W2001-02245-SC-R11-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT JACKSON


118 S.W.3d 360; 2003 Tenn. LEXIS 983


June 4, 2003, Session, Heard at Nashville  
October 20, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 20, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Appeal by Permission; Judgment of the Court of Appeals Reversed; Case Remanded to Trial Court. Appeal by Permission from the Court of Appeals. Chancery Court for Shelby County. No. CH-01-0200-2. Kenny W. Armstrong, Special Chancellor.
In re D.L.B., 2002 Tenn. App. LEXIS 575 (Tenn. Ct. App., Aug. 6, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, adoptive parents, filed an action to terminate the parental rights of respondent father, on the basis that he abandoned the subject child, pursuant to Tenn. Code Ann. § 36-1-102(1)(A)(i). The Chancery Court for Shelby County, Tennessee, entered an order for termination. The Tennessee Court of Appeals affirmed and found the father's failure to pay support as an additional ground for termination. By permission, the father appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mother's parental rights were already terminated, largely as a consequence of her cocaine addiction. The Tennessee Supreme Court held that only a parent's conduct in the four months immediately preceding the filing of a petition then before the trial court could be used as grounds to terminate parental rights under Tenn. Code Ann. § 36-1-102(1)(A)(i). As there was no dispute that the father paid child support and visited the child in the four months preceding the filing of the petition, the lower courts erred in terminating the father's parental rights based upon § 36-1-102(1)(A)(i). Further, the court of appeals erred in terminating the father's rights based upon Tenn. Code Ann. § 36-1-102(1)(A)(iii) (relevant to paying reasonable support), because the court of appeals did not address the required element of willfulness. Moreover, the trial court, not the court of appeals, was the proper court to make a determination of willfulness pursuant to § 36-1-102(1)(A)(iii). Finally, the trial court failed to address each ground for termination of parental rights that was asserted. Thus, a remand on the issue of abandonment and any other grounds for termination was necessary.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, and the matter was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings, consistent with the supreme court's decision.

Doe v. Bd. of Prof'l Responsibility of the Supreme Court of Tenn.


No. M2002-02076-SC-R23-CQ


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


104 S.W.3d 465; 2003 Tenn. LEXIS 371



May 8, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 5, 2003. As Corrected May 13, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. Sup. Ct. R. 23 Certified Question of Law. United States District Court for the Western District of Tennessee. Hon. Julia Smith Gibbons, Judge.
Doe v. Bd. of Prof'l Responsibility, 2002 Tenn. LEXIS 713 (Tenn., Dec. 23, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Certified question answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In federal court, plaintiff non-lawyer sued defendant Board of Professional Responsibility of the Supreme Court of Tennessee (Board), challenging the constitutionality of Tenn. Sup. Ct. R. 9, § 25. The United States District Court for the Western District of Tennessee certified questions of law regarding the construction of Rule 9, § 25.


OVERVIEW: The non-lawyer filed a complaint with the Board against an attorney and desired to speak publicly about the complaint. In the non-lawyer's constitutionality suit, the Board argued that the non-lawyer lacked standing. The federal court sought to know whether the non-lawyer could be charged with contempt for disclosing that he filed a complaint with the Board against an attorney in violation of the confidentiality provision embodied in Tenn. Sup. Ct. R. 9, § 25, and, if so, by whom and before what tribunal. The state court determined that, inter alia: (1) the confidentiality requirement applied to a layperson who filed a complaint against an attorney with the Board; (2) an appropriate sanction for a violation of Rule 9, § 25 was an action for contempt; (3) an action for contempt may be initiated by any person whose rights under Rule 9, § 25 were violated; (4) the Supreme Court of Tennessee or the Board could pursue an action for contempt; and (5) a charge of contempt was to be filed first with the Supreme Court of Tennessee, whereupon assignment shall issue to special master to make findings of fact.


OUTCOME: The court accepted certification and construed the rule at issue.

Gallaher v. Elam


No. E2000-02719-SC-R11-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT KNOXVILLE


104 S.W.3d 455; 2003 Tenn. LEXIS 337



May 2, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Appeal by Permission; Judgment of the Court of Appeals Reversed; Case Remanded. Appeal by Permission from the Court of Appeals Juvenile Court for Knox County. No. B-3443. Carey E. Garrett, Judge.
Gallaher v. Elam, 2002 Tenn. App. LEXIS 94 (Tenn. Ct. App., Jan. 29, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother filed a petition for contempt against appellee father for his failure to comply with a child support agreement. The Court of Appeals Juvenile Court for Knox County, Tennessee, agreed with the trial court and held that Tenn. Dep't Human Serv. Child Support Guidelines, ch. 1240-2-4-.03(4) violated the equal protection provisions of the state and federal constitutions. The mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The mother brought a contempt action against the father, as he failed to comply with an agreed judgment regarding child support and also requested an increase in child support. The father responded by arguing that the Tennessee Department of Human Services Child Support Guidelines (Guidelines) were unconstitutional and violated the equal protection clauses of the state and federal constitutions, as they prohibited consideration of non-court ordered child support and required only consideration of the obligor's income in calculating support. The state supreme court reversed the court of appeals and held, as the Guidelines did not impact a fundamental right, the court's inquiry was whether the Guidelines bore a reasonable relation to a proper legislative purpose and were neither arbitrary nor discriminatory. The state supreme court held that: (1) the Guidelines did not violate the equal protection and due process provisions of either the United States or Tennessee Constitutions; and (2) the promulgation of the Guidelines did not constitute an impermissible delegation of rulemaking authority by the Tennessee General Assembly to the Tennessee Department of Human Services.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, and the matter was remanded to the trial court for the computation of the child support. Attorney fees at the trial level and on appeal were awarded to the mother.

State v. Reid

91 S.W.3d 247; 2002 Tenn. LEXIS 550



November 26, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Reid v. Tennessee, 540 U.S. 828, 124 S. Ct. 56, 157 L. Ed. 2d 52, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 6131 (2003)
Subsequent appeal at State v. Reid, 2003 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 1086 (Tenn. Crim. App., Dec. 29, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-206(a)(1) Automatic Appeal; Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed. Direct Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Davidson County. No. 97-C-1834. Cheryl Blackburn, Judge.
State v. Reid, 2001 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 413 (Tenn. Crim. App., May 31, 2001)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first-degree murder and one count of especially aggravated robbery. He was sentenced to death on each murder conviction and a 25-year sentence was imposed for the robbery conviction to be served consecutively to the two death sentences. The Tennessee of Criminal Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.

The sentences of death were not imposed arbitrarily and were not excessive or disproportionate, considering the nature of the crime.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentences were affirmed.

Martin v. Lear Corp.


No. E2001-01002-SC-WCM-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT KNOXVILLE


90 S.W.3d 626; 2002 Tenn. LEXIS 459



October 24, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. Code Ann. § 50-6-225(e); Appeal from the Circuit Court for Hamblen County. No. 98-CV-303. Kindall Lawson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Trial Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this workers' compensation case, the Supreme Court of Tennessee was called upon to determine whether the trial court properly admitted a C-32 form medical report obtained by the claimant, an injured employee, by the defendant employer's consulting expert, a physician who made a court ordered physical examination of the injured employee. The trial court admitted the medical report, and the employer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The claimant worked for the employer. When he began to experience pain, he reported it to his supervisor. He was diagnosed with carpal tunnel syndrome. He underwent surgery. As a result of his employment, he suffered from depression, sleeplessness, and constant disabling pain, which prevented him from performing many day-to-day activities. The employer requested a court ordered independent medical examination by a neurologist. The motion was granted, and the examination was performed. The claimant filed a notice that he would file the doctor's form C-32 medical report, to be used as evidence under Tenn. Code Ann. § 50-6-235(c)(1). The employer objected, claiming Tenn. R. Civ. P. 26.02(4)(B) protected from discovery the opinion of a consulting expert who would not be called as a witness. However, Tenn. Code Ann. §§ 50-6- 204(f) and 50-6-235(c) specifically permitted admission of the testimony, including a medical report form, of an examining physician paid for by the employer in a workers' compensation case. Therefore, the instant court held, pursuant to Tenn. Code Ann. §§ 50-6-204(f) and 50-6-235(c), that the trial court properly admitted the physician's medical report.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Tenn. Small Sch. Sys. v. McWherter


No. M2001-01957-SC-R3-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT NASHVILLE


91 S.W.3d 232; 2002 Tenn. LEXIS 425; 110 A.L.R.5th 707



 October 8, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal Pursuant to Tenn. Code Ann. § 16-3-201; Judgment of the Trial Court Reversed and Remanded. Appeal pursuant to Tenn. Code Ann. § 16-3-201 from the Chancery Court for Davidson County. No. 88-1812-II. Carol L. McCoy, Chancellor



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Trial Court reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The case at bar was the third plaintiff teachers' suit challenging the constitutionality of the manner in which the State funded public education. The question was whether the State's current method of funding salaries for teachers, the salary equity plan found in Tenn. Code Ann. § 49-3-366, violated equal protection. The Chancery Court for Davidson County (Tennessee) dismissed the case. The teachers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The teachers claimed that the State failed to comply with the Supreme Court of Tennessee's directive in Small Schools II to equalize teachers' salaries according to the basic education plan (BEP) formula for funding public education. After careful consideration of the record and applicable authorities, the court found that the salary equity plan embodied in § 49-3-366 did not equalize teachers' salaries according to the BEP formula and contained no mechanism for cost determination or annual cost review of teachers' salaries, unlike the BEP conditionally approved in Small Schools II. The court found that no rational basis existed for structuring a basic education program consisting entirely of cost-driven components while omitting the cost of hiring teachers, the most important component of any education plan. The lack of teacher salary equalization in accordance with the BEP formula continued to be a significant constitutional defect in the funding scheme. The salary equity plan failed to comply with the State's constitutional obligation to formulate and maintain a system of public education that afforded a substantially equal educational opportunity to all students.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and the case was remanded.

State v. Austin

SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT JACKSON

US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Austin v. Tennessee, 538 U.S. 1001, 123 S. Ct. 1899, 155 L. Ed. 2d 829, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 3289 (2003)
Related proceeding at Austin v. State, 2006 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 970 (Tenn. Crim. App., Dec. 13, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-206(a)(1); Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed. Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Shelby County. No. B-58357. C. Creed McGinley, Judge, by Designation.
State v. Austin, 2001 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 162 (Tenn. Crim. App., Mar. 6, 2001)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals affirmed the trial court's sentencing of defendant to death, following his conviction for premeditated murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued the trial court committed reversible error in excluding certain mitigating evidence and in admitting victim impact evidence; he argued that the death sentence was disproportionate. The appellate court found that hearsay was admissible in a capital sentencing hearing. The death sentence was not imposed in an arbitrary fashion. Because neither codefendant was subjected to capital proceedings, their cases were not similar cases for purposes of proportionality review. Defendant's case was proportionate when compared to other murders for hire in which the death penalty was imposed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

It agreed that the corporation was an independent contractor, not a governmental agency, but held the trial court's grant of access to all records overbroad. The instant court held all the corporation's records were subject to access under the Tennessee Public Records Act.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the opinion.

State v. Torres


No. E1999-00866-SC-DDT-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, AT KNOXVILLE


82 S.W.3d 236; 2002 Tenn. LEXIS 336



July 19, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-206(a)(1) Automatic Appeal; Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed in Part; Reversed in Part; Case Remanded to the Trial Court. Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Knox County. No. 56073. Ray L. Jenkins, Judge.
 State v. Torres, 2001 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 180 (Tenn. Crim. App. Mar. 13, 2001)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder by aggravated child abuse. Defendant was sentenced to death. The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred during the sentencing phase of the trial by giving the jury a Kersey instruction. The supreme court agreed. The jury deliberated for approximately six hours on the sentencing issue before reporting: "We are at a deadlock, 11 for death, and 1 for life imprisonment. What do we do at this point? The one for life imprisonment has stated that he will not change his mind." Over defense objections, the trial court returned the jury to open court and gave the instruction, the trial court did not ask the jurors whether further instructions and deliberations might assist them in returning a verdict. Instead, the trial court simply gave the instruction and ordered the jury to continue deliberation. Just one hour later, the jury returned with a unanimous verdict of death. While no inquiry was made, the note informed the trial court of the jury's division on the sentence of death, and the dissenting juror, who presumably held out for six hours, likely concluded that the trial court was instructing "him," one of only four men on the jury, to reconsider his position. Therefore, the trial court erred.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for first degree murder was affirmed. The sentence of death was vacated, and the case was remanded for a new sentencing hearing.

OUTCOME: The judgments of the trial and appellate courts were affirmed.

Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by McKinney v. State, 2010 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 219 (Tenn. Crim. App., Mar. 9, 2010)

State v. McKinney, 2001 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 230 (Tenn. Crim. App., Mar. 28, 2001)

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The case was automatically docketed in the supreme court after the Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals affirmed the judgment of a jury of the Criminal Court for Shelby County (Tennessee), convicting defendant of premeditated first degree murder and attempted second degree murder, and imposing a death sentence for the first degree murder and a 12 year sentence for the attempted second degree murder, to run consecutively to the death sentence.

OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of criminal appeals was affirmed.

 

149 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


State v. Taylor


No. M1999-02566-SC-R11-CD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, MIDDLE SECTION, AT NASHVILLE


70 S.W.3d 717; 2002 Tenn. LEXIS 145


February 6, 2002, Assigned on Briefs  
March 19, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication March 19, 2002.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Appeal by Permission; Judgment of the Court of Appeals Reversed in Part; Affirmed in Part. Appeal by permission from the Court of Criminal Appeals. Coffee Circuit, No. 28970. L. Craig Johnson, Judge.

State v. Taylor, 2001 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 311 (Tenn. Crim. App. Apr. 26, 2001).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of driving under the influence (DUI), second offense, and driving on a revoked license (DRL), second offense, and imposed fines of § 3,500 and $ 27,500 respectively. The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals held that former Tenn. Code Ann. § 55-50-504(a)(2) violated Tenn. Const. art. I, § 16 and U.S. Const. amend. VIII, because it did not specify the maximum fine for a DRL conviction. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Former Tenn. Code Ann. § 55-50- 504(a)(2) provided a minimum fine for second offense driving on a revoked license but failed to provide a maximum fine. The supreme court noted that when considering the constitutionality of a statute, courts had a duty to adopt a construction which would sustain the statute and avoid constitutional conflict if at all possible, and this duty required courts to indulge every presumption and resolve every doubt in favor of the statute's constitutionality. The supreme court concluded that the statute was not facially unconstitutional under either the state or the federal constitution for the mere failure to fix a maximum fine. However, fines imposed in specific cases under such statutes were subject to being challenged as excessive. When such a challenge was made, however, courts were required to first apply the principles of the Tennessee Criminal Sentencing Reform Act of 1989, Tenn. Code Ann. § 40-35-101 et seq., to determine whether the fine was excessive before analyzing the constitutional validity of the fine. The supreme court held the $ 27,500 fine imposed by the jury was excessive under the principles of the Act.


OUTCOME: The supreme court held the court of criminal appeals erred in holding the subject statute unconstitutional and modified defendant's fine for driving on a revoked license, second offense, to the statutory minimum of $ 3,000. Costs of the appeal were taxed equally between the State and defendant.

State v. Walls


No. W2000-00637-SC-R11-CD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, WESTERN SECTION, AT JACKSON


62 S.W.3d 119; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 836


October 5, 2001, Argued  
December 11, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal by Permission from the Court of Criminal Appeals. Criminal Court for Shelby County. Nos. 98-03965, 98-03958. Chris Craft, Judge.

State v. Walls, 2001 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 107 (Tenn. Crim. App. Feb. 15, 2001) 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Criminal Appeals, Tennessee, affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of felony escape from a penal institution and theft of property over $ 1,000. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the evidence was insufficient because the legislature did not intend for a police patrol car to be considered a penal institution for the offense of escape under Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-16-605. The language of Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-16-601(4) (1997) did not specifically include or exclude in its definition of a "penal institution" the rear compartment of a police patrol car. There was no indication that the legislature used the word "facility" in any broader context than described in the statute and simply no evidence that it intended the word to include police patrol cars. The apparent similarities in the offenses of resisting arrest, evading arrest, and escape, underscored the importance of ascertaining the legislative intent and purpose in interpreting and applying each offense. Including a patrol car in the definition of "penal institution" unnecessarily expanded the offense of escape to circumstances that the legislature intended to address under other statutory offenses.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

151 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


Van Tran v. State


No. W2000-00739-SC-R11-PD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, WESTERN SECTION, AT JACKSON


66 S.W.3d 790; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 820



Filed December 4, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at Van Tran v. State, 2006 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 899 (Tenn. Crim. App., Nov. 9, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal by permission from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Shelby County. No. P-14409. John P. Colton, Judge.
State v. Heck Van Tran, 864 S.W.2d 465, 1993 Tenn. LEXIS 343 (Tenn., 1993)



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of three counts of felony murder and sentenced to death on one count. Defendant's postconviction relief petition was dismissed. Dismissal was affirmed. He moved to reopen his petition under Tenn. Code Ann. § 40-30-217(a)(2) (1997), which the trial court denied. The Court of Criminal Appeals denied him permission to appeal. The Tennessee Supreme Court granted his application for permission to appeal.


OVERVIEW: While this case was pending on direct appeal, the Tennessee General Assembly enacted Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-203 (1991), which, adopting the national standard of mental retardation, provided that no defendant with mental retardation at the time of committing first degree murder should be sentenced to death. Both before and after defendant's postconviction hearing intelligence tests were administered to him and the testing doctor concluded he was mentally retarded. The supreme court, out of fundamental fairness, treated his motion to reopen as one, more appropriately, under Tenn. Code Ann. § 40-30-217(a)(1), on retrospective application of a new constitutional right. Whether execution of the mentally retarded was cruel and unusual punishment was an issue of first impression for that court, which held that it was cruel and unusual. The federal government and 18 states had legislation that prohibited their execution, and 12 states did not provide for capital punishment at all, showing that such executions were against contemporary standards of decency. Also under U.S. Const. amend. VIII and Tenn. Const. art. I, § 16 analysis, execution of the mentally retarded was disproportional.


OUTCOME: The judgments of the trial court and the court of criminal appeals were reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings on the question of whether defendant was mentally retarded.

 

152 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


State v. Godsey


No. E1997-00207-SC-R11-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, EASTERN SECTION, AT KNOXVILLE


60 S.W.3d 759; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 809



November 29, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal by permission from the Court of Criminal Appeals. Criminal Court for Sullivan County. No. S38648. R. Jerry Beck, Judge.

State v. Godsey, 2000 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 723 (Tenn. Crim. App. Sept. 18, 2000) 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of aggravated child abuse and first degree murder by aggravated child abuse, and was sentenced to death. The Court of Criminal Appeals (Tennessee) vacated the abuse conviction, and found the death sentence disproportionate and modified it to life imprisonment without the possibility of parole. On further appeal, defendant argued against his conviction and the State argued against the sentence modification.


OVERVIEW: Defendant lived with his girlfriend and her children, the youngest seven months old, and often babysat them. One night she left them alone with him for a few minutes. A few hours later defendant discovered the baby not breathing and tried to resuscitate him. The baby, with no external bleeding or large bruising, but with skull fractures, soon died. After initial denials and differing accounts, defendant admitted to having become angry and physically abusing the baby. The supreme court held that electronic recording of interrogations was not required. Both the statutory elements and the case's facts established reckless indifference. As aggravated child abuse was a felony capable of supporting a first degree felony murder conviction, the merger doctrine was not to be applied to preclude that conviction even though the abuse resulted in the victim's death. This abuse was not a lesser included offense; the dual convictions were permissible. The Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-13-204(i)(1) aggravator sufficiently narrowed the class of death-eligible defendants. Unlike compared cases, defendant may not have immediately realized the extent of the injuries. He appeared amenable to rehabilitation.


OUTCOME: The vacation of the aggravated child abuse conviction was reversed and that part of the trial court judgment was reinstated. The sentence modification was affirmed.

State v. Burkhart


No. M1998-00344-SC-R11-CD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, MIDDLE SECTION, AT NASHVILLE


58 S.W.3d 694; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 733



October 10, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied March 18, 2002, Reported at: 2002 U.S. LEXIS 1590.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by Permission from the Court of Criminal Appeals Circuit Court for Montgomery County. No. 38568. John H. Gasaway, III, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with possession of a gambling device, in violation of Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-17-505 (1989). The trial court granted her motion to dismiss. The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals reversed the trial court's judgment, and defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Slot machines were confiscated from defendant. The machines recorded games or credits won by a player. Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-17-501's predecessor, Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-6-601(4) (1982, repealed), excluded from the definition of a gambling device machines which entitled a player to replay a game at no additional cost if (1) it accumulated and reacted to no more than 15 free replays; (2) discharged free replays only by reactivating the device for an additional play for each free replay; and (3) made no permanent record of free replays. The Sentencing Commission Comments to § 39-17-501 provided the statute's definitions were intentionally broader than those found in the prior law. Since the earlier definition did not encompass defendant's devices, the current statute also applied to them, and defendant was on notice they were prohibited. Speculative danger of arbitrary enforcement of § 39-17-501 did not render it void for vagueness. Since possession of a gambling device was not constitutionally protected conduct, § 39-17-501, outlawing their possession, did not reach a substantial amount of such conduct and was not overbroad.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of criminal appeals was affirmed.

Gibson v. Trant


No. M1999-00390-SC-R11-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, MIDDLE SECTION, AT NASHVILLE


58 S.W.3d 103; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 722



October 5, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [**1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Knox County. No. 3-399-93. Wheeler A. Rosenbalm, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Appeals Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff inmate filed a civil complaint alleging defendant lawyers' misconduct in his federal criminal trial constituted legal malpractice. The Tennessee Court of Appeals affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the inmate's malpractice claim. The inmate appealed to the instant court.


OVERVIEW: The inmate, along with other co-defendants, was charged with committing drug-related offenses in a multi-count indictment in federal district court. He hired the lawyers to represent him. He later alleged the lawyers asked him to persuade his co-defendants to hire them as well, telling him that none of the co-defendants could plead out and testify against him if they were under the lawyers' representation. Seven co-defendants hired the lawyers. The inmate alleged this joint representation created a conflict of interest. He also alleged the lawyers pressured him to plead guilty. After the inmate was sentenced to 20 years in prison, he filed a civil legal malpractice claim in state court. At the same time, his "post-conviction" motion to have his plea vacated on the ground of involuntariness was denied. The malpractice action was later dismissed. The appeals court concluded that because the federal courts had already determined the inmate's plea was voluntary, this finding necessarily encompassed the rejection of his malpractice allegations. If the evidence showed he pled guilty not because he was guilty but because of threats, the federal courts would have granted him relief.


OUTCOME: The Tennessee Supreme Court affirmed the judgment of the Tennessee Court of Appeals.

 

155 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hicks


No. E1999-00957-SC-R11-CD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, EASTERN SECTION, AT KNOXVILLE


55 S.W.3d 515; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 658



September 11, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal by Permission from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Hamilton County. No. 221717. Hon. Douglas A. Meyer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Application for Permission to Appeal; Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Reversed; Indictment Dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to suppress marijuana seized from his vehicle when he was stopped at a roadblock, and the trial court granted his motion. The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals reversed that judgment, and defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: A city police officer stopped defendant at a roadblock purportedly established to check drivers' licenses, and, marijuana was discovered in his vehicle. The statutory authority of highway patrol officers present to stop a vehicle to check operators' licenses was impermissibly delegated to other officers. The State presented no evidence of a required compelling state interest in maintaining the roadblock, and none was presumed, nor was there evidence it advanced such an interest. There was no evidence of any genuine limitations on the discretion of the officers in the field, and the roadblock was operated with little regard for the safety of approaching motorists. There was no advance publicity of the roadblock, nor was there any showing it was established with prior administrative authority. It did not comply with existing administrative guidelines from the department of safety. The presence of a drug-sniffing dog, and other evidence, indicated the roadblock was actually a subterfuge for other objectives.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of criminal appeals was reversed, and the indictment against the defendant was dismissed.

 

156 of 1427 DOCUMENTS


City of Chattanooga v. Davis


No. E2000-00664-SC-R11-CV, No. M1999-01130-SC-R11-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, MIDDLE SECTION, AT NASHVILLE


54 S.W.3d 248; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 635



September 4, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal by Permission from the Court of Appeals Criminal Court for Hamilton County. No. 225103. Hon. Douglas A. Meyer, Judge. Appeal by Permission from the Court of Appeals Circuit Court for Davidson County. No. 98C-1095. Hon. Walter C. Kurtz, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Application for Permission to Appeal; Judgment of the Court of Appeals Affirmed in Part, and Affirmed in Part as Modified in City of Chattanooga v. Davis; Judgment of the Court of Appeals Reversed in Barrett v. Metropolitan Government.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two separate cases involving municipal codes, the Court of Appeals (Tennessee) ruled that a $ 300 sanction imposed by appellee city for reckless driving did not violate Tenn. Const. art. VI, § 14, but affirmed reduction of the fine and found that Tenn. Code Ann. §§ 6-54-306, 55-10-307 were constitutional; and ruled that an assessment's label was immaterial to whether it fell within § 14. Appellants, citizens, received consolidated review.


OVERVIEW: No other provision like § 14, prohibiting any fine greater than $ 50 unless assessed by jury, was either in the federal or any other modern state constitution. The city court assessed a citizen for reckless driving under the authority of Chattanooga, Tenn., City Code §§ 24-13(a), 1-8(a). The county court, under the authority of Nashville and Davidson County, Tenn., Metropolitan Code of Laws § 16.04.172(A), assessed a citizen $ 500 for failures to secure a building permit and abide by a stop-work order. The Tennessee Supreme Court held that § 14 applied to proceedings involving municipal ordinance violations when either the intended or actual purpose or effect of the monetary assessment was to serve as punishment. Because § 14 looked to the substance of a pecuniary sanction rather than its technical form, technical distinctions as to the nature of the proceeding wherein the sanction was imposed and the sanction's label would not be made. O'Dell v. City of Knoxville, 388 S.W.2d 150 (Tenn. Ct. App. 1964), was overruled to the extent it held otherwise. The assessments were punitive. The citizens lacked standing to challenge city usurpation of district attorney discretion.


OUTCOME: In one case, the affirmation of a $ 50 rather than $ 300 fine was effectively upheld, as modified, by the holding that the $ 300 city court fine had been unconstitutionally imposed. In the other case, the judgment was reversed and the citizen's five fines were reduced to $ 50 for each warrant.

OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial, the jury was instructed that the required first-degree murder element of premeditation could be formed in an instant. Post-conviction relief denied at Bane v. State, 2011 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 574 (Tenn. Crim. App., July 21, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Automatic Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals Criminal Court for Shelby County. No. 89-01502. John P. Colton, Jr., Judge.
State v. Bane, 2000 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 56 (Tenn. Crim. App., Jan. 24, 2000)



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the trial court convicted defendant of murder, found that there was evidence supporting two aggravating circumstances, and imposed a sentence of death. The Court of Criminal Appeals, Criminal Court for Shelby County, Tennessee, affirmed the trial court's judgment. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that an accomplice could not testify regarding aggravating circumstances without corroborating testimony. The supreme court concluded that there was no basis or rationale for applying the corroboration requirement to the sentencing phase of a capital trial. Finally, the supreme court found that the evidence supported the jury's finding of the statutory aggravating circumstances; that the evidence supported the jury's finding that the aggravating circumstances outweighed the mitigating circumstances; and that the sentence was not arbitrary, excessive, or disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the criminal court of appeals' judgment.

Terry v. State


No. M1999-00191-SC-DDT-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, MIDDLE SECTION, AT NASHVILLE


46 S.W.3d 147; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 370



April 25, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 14, 2001. Rehearing Denied May 18, 2001, Reported at: 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 445. Certiorari Denied November 13, 2001, Reported at: 2001 U.S. LEXIS 10406.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Automatic Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals. Criminal Court for Davidson County No. 87-S-975. Hon. Randall Wyatt, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of the Court of Criminal Appeals Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and arson and sentenced to death, then granted a new sentencing hearing. The trial court found the Tenn. Code Ann. § 39-2-203(i)(5) and (6) (1982) aggravators of "heinous, atrocious, and cruel" and "avoiding arrest," and resentenced defendant to death. The Court of Criminal Appeals (Tennessee) affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, pastor, took church funds starting in 1984. Defendant got depressed in March 1987 when his superior, whose position he coveted, opted not to retire. Defendant stole more money, procured false identification papers, befriended a man who was his height, and hired him as church handyman, all as part of a plan to disappear and assume a new identity. In June, defendant shot the man to death; cut off his head and arm; left defendant's wallet in, and car with his own blood near, the victim's boarding room; and burned the church building containing the victim's body. After two days, defendant, very different in appearance, was caught. Defendant was convicted and sentenced. The Supreme Court held that the State's closing argument about "unique circumstances" was not an improper presentation of non-statutory aggravators in that the State first identified the two aggravators then offered factors to establish them. Defendant's extensive plan singling out the victim, and the dismemberment, supported the "heinous" aggravator. The jury could have found that defendant murdered, in part, to avoid arrest for his separate theft crime. Despite no prior record, sentence was proportional.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

In re Adoption of Female Child


No. M1998-00417-SC-R11-CV


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, MIDDLE SECTION, AT NASHVILLE


42 S.W.3d 26; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 287



April 6, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appeal by Permission from the Court of Appeals, Middle Section Chancery Court for Lawrence County. No. 8663-97. Robert L. Jones, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Tenn. R. App. P. 11 Appeal by Permission; Judgment of the Court of Appeals Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs filed a petition to terminate defendant's parental rights. At the same time, defendant filed a petition to legitimize the child. The trial court held that the best interests of the child would be served by termination of defendant's parental rights. The Tennessee Court of Appeals refused to address the issue of whether Tenn. Code Ann. § 36-1-113(g)(6) was constitutional. Defendant requested review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with the April 1992 aggravated rape of a five-year-old girl. Defendant began dating plaintiff, who was later to become the mother of his child. Plaintiff mother married plaintiff stepfather in 1997. During closing argument at trial, defendant's counsel, for the first time, claimed that Tenn. Code Ann. §§ 36-1-113(g)(6), 36-1-113(c)(2) were unconstitutional. The supreme court's review of the record showed that the trial court never affirmatively decided the issue of constitutionality, either orally from the bench or in its written order, and certainly never provided citation to any authority for that supposed ruling. Defendant failed to timely raise a challenge to the constitutionality of the two statutes. His challenge was, therefore, waived except to the extent that the statutes were clearly or blatantly unconstitutional. The statutes at issue in the case were not clearly or blatantly unconstitutional. Moreover, failure to timely raise the issue at trial was compounded by the fact that the Tennessee Attorney General was not properly informed of the constitutional challenge as required by statute and rule.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Workman v. State


No. W2001-00774-SC-R11-PD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, MIDDLE SECTION, AT NASHVILLE


41 S.W.3d 100; 2001 Tenn. LEXIS 306



March 29, 2001, Decided  
March 30, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied November 5, 2001.
Appeal after remand at State v. Workman, 111 S.W.3d 10, 2002 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 1124 (Tenn. Crim. App., 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Court of Criminal Appeals. Criminal Court for Shelby County. John P. Colton, Jr., Judge. No. B81209, P-3908, and P-3841.
Workman v. Bell, 245 F.3d 849, 2001 U.S. App. LEXIS 4898 (6th Cir.) (6th Cir., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Decision reversed and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, sentenced to death, petitioned for writ of error coram nobis alleging new evidence. The petition was filed after the expiration of the applicable statute of limitations, Tenn. Code Ann. § 40-26-105. The trial court dismissed the petition as untimely. The Tennessee Court of Criminal Appeals affirmed, and defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: While the statute of limitations in Tenn. Code Ann. § 40-26-105, regarding filing of a petition for post-conviction relief, was not unconstitutional on its face, it could be unconstitutional as applied if it denied a defendant due process. A potential litigant had to be provided an opportunity to present a claim at a meaningful time and in a meaningful manner before terminating his claim for failure to comply with a statute of limitations. This applied with even greater force when applied in a capital case to bar a claim newly discovered evidence might prove a defendant was innocent. Consideration of both the governmental interests involved and the private interests affected by the official action was required in determining what process was due. The governmental interest was the prevention of stale and groundless claims. The private interest was the opportunity to have a hearing on the grounds of newly discovered evidence which might have resulted in a different verdict if heard by the jury at trial. Application of the procedural time bar would result in defendant being put to death without an opportunity to have the merits of his claim evaluated by a court.


OUTCOME: The judgments of the court of criminal appeals and the trial court were reversed as defendant's interest in presenting his claim outweighed the government's interest. Defendant had to have an opportunity to show new evidence might have resulted in a different trial judgment. If he also showed no fault in failing to present the new evidence at the proper time, he would be entitled to a new trial.

LensCrafters, Inc. v. Sundquist


No. M1999-01349-SC-R23-CQ


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, MIDDLE SECTION, AT NASHVILLE


33 S.W.3d 772; 2000 Tenn. LEXIS 688



December 13, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certified Question from the United States District Court for the Middle District of Tennessee. No. 3:98-0150. Aleta Trauger, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation brought a cause of action against defendant governor challenging the constitutionality of Tenn. Code. Ann. § 63-8-113(c)(6). The district court certified the following question for the court's determination: Whether an entity engaged primarily in the business of selling and dispensing ophthalmic lenses and frames is a retail store or other commercial establishment under Tenn. Code Ann. § 63-8-113(c)(6)?


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation owned numerous optical superstores throughout the country. The Tennessee Board of Optometry sanctioned a licensed optometrist who leased space from plaintiff for violating Tenn. Code Ann. § 63-8-113(c)(6) (1997) by practicing in conjunction with a retail store where merchandise was displayed or offered for sale. Plaintiff brought a cause of action against defendant governor challenging the constitutionality of Tenn. Code. Ann. § 63-8-113(c)(6) (1997). The trial court certified the following question for the court: Whether an entity engaged primarily in the business of selling and dispensing ophthalmic lenses and frames is a retail store or other commercial establishment within the meaning of Tenn. Code Ann. § 63-8-113(c)(6)? The court answered in the affirmative. Businesses which sell ophthalmic lenses and frames are retail establishments. The purpose of Tenn. Code Ann. § 63-8-1139(c)(6) was to prevent the compromising of the optometrists' professional autonomy by forbidding optometrists from forming business association with non-health care provider commercial entities.


OUTCOME: For the purposes of the statute in question, an entity engaged primarily in the business of selling and dispensing ophthalmic lenses and frames was considered to be a retail store. The statute was designed to prevent the compromising of the optometrists' professional autonomy.

State v. Carruthers


No. W1997-00097-SC-DDT-DD


SUPREME COURT OF TENNESSEE, WESTERN SECTION, AT JACKSON


35 S.W.3d 516; 2000 Tenn. LEXIS 683



December 11, 2000, Filed


NOTICE:

[EDITOR'S NOTE: PART 2 OF 2. THIS DOCUMENT HAS BEEN SPLIT INTO MULTIPLE PARTS ON LEXIS TO ACCOMMODATE ITS LARGE SIZE. EACH PART CONTAINS THE SAME LEXIS CITE.]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Certiorari Denied June 29, 2001, Reported at: 2001 U.S. LEXIS 5032.
Writ of certiorari denied Carruthers v. Tennessee, 533 U.S. 953, 121 S. Ct. 2600, 150 L. Ed. 2d 757, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 5032 (2001)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by Carruthers v. State, 145 S.W.3d 85, 2003 Tenn. Crim. App. LEXIS 839 (Tenn. Crim. App., 2003)
